</div>
</div></section></section><section aria-label="Article content"><section class="body main-article-body"><section class="abstract" id="abstract1"><h2>Abstract</h2>
<p>In recent years high-THC (psychoactive) and low-THC (industrial hemp) type cannabis (<em>Cannabis sativa</em> L.) have gained immense attention in medical, food, and a plethora of other consumer product markets. Among the planting materials used for cultivation, tissue culture clones provide various advantages such as economies of scale, production of disease-free and true-to-type plants for reducing the risk of GMP-EuGMP level medical cannabis production, as well as the development and application of various technologies for genetic improvement. Various tissue culture methods have the potential application with cannabis for research, breeding, and novel trait development, as well as commercial mass propagation. Although tissue culture techniques for plant regeneration and micropropagation have been reported for different cannabis genotypes and explant sources, there are significant variations in the response of cultures and the morphogenic pathway. Methods for many high-yielding elite strains are still rudimentary, and protocols are not established. With a recent focus on sequencing and genomics in cannabis, genetic transformation systems are applied to medical cannabis and hemp for functional gene annotation via traditional and transient transformation methods to create novel phenotypes by gene expression modulation and to validate gene function. This review presents the current status of research focusing on different aspects of tissue culture, including micropropagation, transformation, and the regeneration of medicinal cannabis and industrial hemp transformants. Potential future tissue culture research strategies helping elite cannabis breeding and propagation are also presented.</p>
<section id="kwd-group1" class="kwd-group"><p><strong>Keywords:</strong> <em>Cannabis sativa</em>, micropropagation, tissue culture, hemp, <em>in vitro</em></p></section></section><section id="S1"><h2 class="pmc_sec_title">Introduction</h2>
<p>Cannabis is a multipurpose crop with nutritional, medicinal, and industrial uses. Its leaves and flowers produce a spectrum of biologically active secondary metabolites, seeds are a source of nutritious oil and protein, and the stem contains two types of fiber serving as feedstock for the manufacturing of a variety of bio-based consumer goods (<a href="#B164" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="B164">Small, 2004</a>; <a href="#B148" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="B148">Rodriguez-Leyva and Pierce, 2010</a>; <a href="#B190" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="B190">Wargent et al., 2013</a>; <a href="#B9" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="B9">Andre et al., 2016</a>; <a href="#B126" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="B126">Musio et al., 2018</a>). The crop may have originated and been domesticated over 5000 years ago in Asia; since then, it has been interwoven with human history. In the South Asian regions, cannabis biotypes with elevated THC levels were commonly used for medicinal and recreational purposes, building a strong connection to social and religious rituals. While in the temperate climates, low-THC types were grown initially for fiber, and later also for food (<a href="#B29" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="B29">Cheng, 1963</a>; <a href="#B105" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="B105">Li, 1974</a>; <a href="#B115" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="B115">Mechoulam, 1986</a>; <a href="#B30" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="B30">Cherney and Small, 2016</a>; <a href="#B33" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="B33">Clarke and Merlin, 2016</a>; <a href="#B86" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="B86">Jiang et al., 2016</a>). Since the discovery of two cannabinoids [cannabidiol (CBD) in 1963) and tetrahydrocannabinol (THC) in 1964] in Dr. Raphael Mechoulam’s laboratory, more than 100 additional phytocannabinoids, flavonoids, and over 150 terpenes have been identified in the plant (<a href="#B9" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="B9">Andre et al., 2016</a>; <a href="#B15" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="B15">Booth and Bohlmann, 2019</a>; <a href="#B144" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="B144">Rea et al., 2019</a>). This high-value crop has built a strong foundation for a multi-billion-dollar global industry. Due to legal restrictions, research and development work has been slow and prevented researchers from investigating cannabis openly and making use of its full potential.</p>
<p>Recent cannabis legalization amendments in Canada, Europe, some parts of the United States, and other parts of the globe have helped promote research and use of this multipurpose crop. Commercial production increased in anticipation and response to the federal legalization of cannabis in Canada in October 2018 under the Cannabis Act (<a href="#B66" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="B66">Government of Canada, 2018</a>). Canada became the second nation after Uruguay (legalized December 2013) to legalize cannabis for recreational use at the federal level (<a href="#B4" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="B4">Adinoff and Reiman, 2019</a>). In the United States, 12 states have legalized cannabis for recreational use, with another 22 legalizing medical use (<a href="#B4" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="B4">Adinoff and Reiman, 2019</a>).</p>
<p>Inherently, cannabis is a dioecious species, with male and female flowers found on separate plants. Monoecious forms, which produce male and female flowers on the same plant, are very seldomly found in nature (<a href="#B33" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="B33">Clarke and Merlin, 2016</a>). Commercial monoecious cultivars of hemp have been bred for oilseed production and improved fiber yield and uniformity that cannot be achieved in dioecious forms exhibiting asynchronous maturation of the stems, as male plants commence an accelerated aging process soon after pollen shed. Due to the dioecious nature of most high THC-type cannabis and the lack of advanced breeding to produce true-to-type seed, they are propagated vegetatively and often grown indoors. Vegetative propagation maintains genetic purity and uniformity among the plants. Traditionally, indoor cannabis cultivators have depended on cuttings from a mother plant to produce genetically similar plants. While cannabis generally roots well (<a href="#B21" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="B21">Caplan et al., 2018</a>) and stem cuttings can produce large numbers of genetically similar plants, this method requires significant amounts of space. It has been observed that plants become less vigorous over time, the mother plants are susceptible to pests and diseases, and the resulting cuttings can harbor unwanted disease and serve as primary inoculum in production spaces.</p>
<p>As an alternative, <em>in vitro</em> techniques offer a promising approach for mass production and germplasm maintenance (<a href="#B195" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="B195">Withers and Engelmann, 1997</a>; <a href="#B191" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="B191">Watt et al., 2000</a>). Micropropagation can facilitate high throughput propagation in many species and forms the basis of disease-free plants for certified clean plant programs (<a href="#B106" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="B106">Lineberger, 1983</a>; <a href="#B8" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="B8">Al-Taleb et al., 2011</a>). Tissue culture based clean plant programs have been used in other vegetatively propagated crops such as potatoes, sweet potato, dates, sugarcane, banana, rice, tobacco, strawberry, grapes, orchids, roses, fruit trees, and some more horticulture of food and ornamental crops, helping to eradicate or prevent the spread of many plant pests, diseases, and viruses (<a href="#B127" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="B127">National Clean Plant Network, 2020</a>). Thus, developing an optimized <em>in vitro</em> method for propagating clean plants is a crucial strategy to produce large-scale genetically identical plants, retain genetic integrity, and maintain the long-term sustainability of the economically valuable crop (<a href="#B34" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="B34">Conway, 2012</a>). This review article aims to provide a comprehensive overview of the most updated available scientific research reported to date on tissue culture in cannabis, to contribute to our understanding of the cannabis tissue culture, and to assess potential applications of the optimized techniques in cannabis plant propagation, regeneration, and transformation.</p></section><section id="S2"><h2 class="pmc_sec_title">Industrial Hemp vs. Medical Cannabis (Marijuana)</h2>
<p>According to <a href="#B168" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="B168">Small et al. (1976)</a>, there are four groups of cannabis, ‘non-intoxicant (some <em>C. sativa</em> accessions),’ ‘semi-intoxicant’ (some <em>C. sativa</em> accessions), ‘intoxicant (<em>C. indica</em>),’ and ‘wild’ (<em>C. ruderalis</em>). <em>Cannabis</em> includes <em>C. indica, C. ruderalis</em>, and <em>C. sativa</em>. However, it has also been proposed that these three groups all belong to a single species (<em>C. sativa</em>) and the taxonomic classification among these proposed species remains a debated issue in <em>Cannabis</em> taxonomy (<a href="#B114" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="B114">McPartland, 2018</a>). For morphological and chemical characters (i.e., floral morphology and THC content), the earlier report considered them as different subspecies (<a href="#B167" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="B167">Small and Cronquist, 1976</a>), while another classified them as different species (<a href="#B77" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="B77">Hillig, 2005</a>).</p>
<p>Further complicating matters is the legal distinction between hemp and drug (narcotic) type cannabis. Any plant containing less than a defined concentration of the psychoactive THC is classified as hemp. In contrast, anything above the critical limit is classified as drug type cannabis. Depending upon the jurisdiction, the threshold THC concatenations in flowering plant parts differentiating between industrial hemp and drug type cannabis range from 0.2% of dry weight in most European counties, which is 0.3% in Canada, United States, and China and Brazil to 1% in Switzerland, Uruguay, Columbia, Mexico, and several Australian states. While this distinction is not based on taxonomy or genetic relationships, several studies have shown that most hemp cultivars are genetically distinct from drug-type cannabis (<a href="#B149" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="B149">Rotherham and Harbison, 2011</a>; <a href="#B23" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="B23">Cascini et al., 2019</a>). Mainly due to legal restrictions, artificial selection influenced by a decade’s long black market, and insufficient knowledge of the <em>Cannabis</em> taxonomy, these sub-types are poorly defined, especially the drug type cannabis.</p>
<p>Hemp is generally cultivated from seed and has named cultivars similar to most other crops. In contrast, drug type cannabis is generally propagated clonally; the clones are often referred to as ‘strains’ but are also often referred to as cultivars. As such, any given strain/cultivar can produce various clonal accessions with dramatically different phenotypes, making names unreliable. Further, many strains are offered by different seed companies, and the degree of genetic similarity or difference among providers has not been quantified; therefore, it is generally expected and accepted that there is significant variation within a single strain among seed companies and even within seed lots. Due to these factors, strain names in drug type cannabis are not reliable regarding a uniform phenotype.</p>
<p><em>Cannabis indica</em> and <em>Cannabis sativa</em> are the major sources of cannabinoids, and are predominantly cultivated, while the third species, <em>C. ruderalis</em> is a wild and hardy species and is rarely grown by cultivators as there is no significant content of cannabinoids (<a href="#B78" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="B78">Hilling and Mahlberg, 2004</a>). In many lay literatures, distinction of ‘indica’ and ‘sativa’ have been mentioned and some of the earlier publications have also gathered some phenotypic differences (<a href="#T1" class="usa-link">Table 1</a> and <a href="#F1" class="usa-link">Figure 1</a>); however, there is neither solid taxonomic agreement nor genetic or chemical evidence supporting the differences (<a href="#B65" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="B65">Gloss, 2015</a>; <a href="#B153" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="B153">Sawler et al., 2015</a>; <a href="#B137" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="B137">Piomelli and Russo, 2016</a>). The use of ‘indica’ and ‘sativa’ is vaguely based on the general notion that ‘sativa’ originated from European hemp, while ‘indica’ originated from the Indian subcontinent (<a href="#B165" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="B165">Small, 2015</a>), but their exact origin is still debatable.</p>

<div class="p text-right font-secondary"><a href="figure/F1/" class="usa-link" target="_blank" rel="noopener noreferrer">Open in a new tab</a></div>
<figcaption><p>Cannabis leaf showing morphological differences of the three different species (<em>C. indica, C. sativa</em>, and <em>C. ruderalis</em>).</p></figcaption></figure></section><section id="S3"><h2 class="pmc_sec_title">Traditional Cloning in Cannabis</h2>
<p>For decades, seed propagation in cannabis has supported agricultural needs and facilitated genetic improvement. However, with modern horticultural practices to the cannabis industry, stem cutting or traditional cloning, and <em>in vitro</em> propagation of this high-value crop has become a common practice (<a href="#B96" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="B96">Lata et al., 2009a</a>,<a href="#B97" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="B97">b</a>, <a href="#B101" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="B101">2011</a>; <a href="#B142" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="B142">Potter, 2009</a>). Other methods of propagation are encapsulation of axillary nodes in calcium alginate beads (<a href="#B96" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="B96">Lata et al., 2009a</a>), leaf derived callus (<a href="#B99" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="B99">Lata et al., 2010c</a>), and temporary immersion bioreactor systems (<a href="#B95" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="B95">Lata et al., 2010b</a>) but these are limited in lab experiments only. Traditional cloning involves taking stem cuttings from a healthy mother plant and providing a rooting environment for the newly cut clone (<a href="#F2" class="usa-link">Figure 2</a>). For selection as a donor, a clear indication of alternating branches with no visible sign of insects, fungus, or any mineral deficiency in a mother plant is required. Cuttings can be taken from any part of a donor; despite some suggestions that growth in the lower half is better, no difference was observed between cuttings taken from the upper and lower part of the plant (<a href="#B21" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="B21">Caplan et al., 2018</a>). However, further research is warranted to test this across more genotypes and conditions. In general, cannabis propagates readily from stem cuttings even without rooting hormones.</p>
<figure class="fig xbox font-sm" id="F2"><h3 class="obj_head">FIGURE 2.</h3>
<p class="img-box line-height-none margin-x-neg-2 tablet:margin-x-0 text-center"><a class="tileshop" target="_blank" href="https://www.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/core/lw/2.0/html/tileshop_pmc/tileshop_pmc_inline.html?title=Click%20on%20image%20to%20zoom&amp;p=PMC3&amp;id=7968383_fpls-12-627240-g002.jpg"><img class="graphic zoom-in" src="https://cdn.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/pmc/blobs/fffd/7968383/4fe1a83c3c7a/fpls-12-627240-g002.jpg" loading="lazy" height="258" width="800" alt="FIGURE 2"></a></p>
<div class="p text-right font-secondary"><a href="figure/F2/" class="usa-link" target="_blank" rel="noopener noreferrer">Open in a new tab</a></div>
<figcaption><p>Hemp nodal cloning. <strong>(A)</strong> Hemp plants at 6–8 leaf stage. <strong>(B)</strong> Elongated lateral branches after terminal buds removed from female plants <strong>(C)</strong> lateral branches planted in soil after excision from mother plants and. <strong>(D)</strong> Vegetative clones transferred to 7-inch pots after roots were established and grown. <strong>(E)</strong> Vegetative clone at maturity.</p></figcaption></figure><p>Stem cuttings have advantages over seed propagation, including quicker maturation, true-to-type plants, and elite genetics maintenance (<a href="#T2" class="usa-link">Table 2</a>). Along with the ease of propagation, the practice can limit unwanted gene flow (<a href="#B112" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="B112">McKey et al., 2010</a>), for example, between the hemp and drug-type, potentially retaining the proportions of active metabolites.</p>

<td valign="top" align="left" rowspan="1" colspan="1">About 2–3 days; cuttings are little easier to root and acclimatize in growing environment</td>
<td valign="top" align="left" rowspan="1" colspan="1">About a week, transition from culture tubes to soil/compost is little riskier</td>
</tr>
<tr>
<td valign="top" align="left" rowspan="1" colspan="1">Cost effectiveness</td>
<td valign="top" align="left" rowspan="1" colspan="1">Can be grown outdoor under little care</td>
<td valign="top" align="left" rowspan="1" colspan="1">Simpler indoor setup</td>
<td valign="top" align="left" rowspan="1" colspan="1">Tissue culture lab investment</td>
</tr>
<tr>
<td valign="top" align="left" rowspan="1" colspan="1">Preferred use</td>
<td valign="top" align="left" rowspan="1" colspan="1">Field</td>
<td valign="top" align="left" rowspan="1" colspan="1">Recreation cannabis</td>
<td valign="top" align="left" rowspan="1" colspan="1">Medical Cannabis</td>
</tr>
</tbody>
</table></div>
<div class="p text-right font-secondary"><a href="table/T2/" class="usa-link" target="_blank" rel="noopener noreferrer">Open in a new tab</a></div>
<div class="tw-foot p"><div class="fn" id="attrib2"><p><em>Information derived from <a href="#B26" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="B26">Chandra et al. (2008</a>, <a href="#B27" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="B27">2015)</a>, <a href="#B20" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="B20">Caplan (2018)</a>, and <a href="#B25" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="B25">Chandra et al. (2020)</a>.</em></p></div></div></section><p>On the downside, space for large scale production is a concern as it can take considerable physical space, representing as much as 20–25% of production space just for cloning. Also, since it is currently manually performed, there is a low multiplication rate, and it is expensive in the long run. Therefore, this technique is more suitable for small growers requiring less than 1000 plants per growth cycle. For this reason, an adaptable, scalable, and robust high throughput tissue culture system with a high multiplication rate which preserves cannabis genetics, and produces more vigorous plants than manual clones, can prove to be more cost-effective in the long run (<a href="#T2" class="usa-link">Table 2</a>). Even small- scale growers with a small budget to use this technique to preserve genetics and test their desired strains’ regenerative capacity as a proof-of-concept. Building a team of experts to develop and execute tissue culture protocols successfully can be expensive and time-intensive initially; however, in the long term, it is a promising tool that has benefited many industries, including horticulture and cereal crops (<a href="#B19" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="B19">Brown and Thorpe, 1995</a>; <a href="#B79" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="B79">Hussain et al., 2012</a>).</p>
<p>Stem cuttings or traditional cloning method is the widely used propagation system adopted by many growers. <em>In vitro</em> propagation is establishing in cannabis industry slowly and is expected to take over the traditional cloning method. Although stem cuttings and <em>in vitro</em> clones can be comparable in terms of vegetative growth and physiological performance (<a href="#B96" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="B96">Lata et al., 2009a</a>), <em>in vitro</em> clones provide many advantages such as faster multiplication rate, clean clones without disease or virus, cost effective etc. (<a href="#T1" class="usa-link">Table 1</a>). Considering these advantages <em>in vitro</em> propagation is expected to become method of choice for propagation as well as genetic preservation in cannabis in the near future.</p></section><section id="S4"><h2 class="pmc_sec_title">Current Utilization and Opportunities for Cannabis Tissue Culture</h2>
<p>The legal hemp for CBD production and the medical cannabis industry is a fast- growing market, and cultivators are turning toward advanced scientific approaches such as <em>in vitro</em> micropropagation, to reduce the production costs and offer scalable, healthy, and high-quality cannabis variety. In addition to a critical need for cost-effective propagation to meet demand, there is also a desire to establish and properly characterize cultivars equivalent to those of traditional agriculture with specific, consistent THC and cannabinoid content to match particular drug and therapeutic requirements. Legalization has opened up the options for accessing more mainstream research applications. This increases the demand for the application of some additional cell technologies applications to this crop.</p>
<section id="S4.SS1"><h3 class="pmc_sec_title">
<em>In vitro</em> Micropropagation</h3>
<p>Although a few hemp cultivars have regenerated <em>in vitro</em> (<a href="#F3" class="usa-link">Figure 3</a>), <em>Cannabis</em> spp. have gained a wide reputation for being recalcitrant to tissue culture. At the beginning of the 1970s, along with the conventional propagation system, <em>in vitro</em> cultures of cannabis were initiated. The majority of the earlier <em>in vitro</em> studies were focused on cannabis callus culture to produce cannabinoids (<a href="#B183" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="B183">Veliky and Genest, 1972</a>; <a href="#B82" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="B82">Itokawa et al., 1975</a>, <a href="#B83" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="B83">1977</a>; <a href="#B74" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="B74">Hemphill et al., 1978</a>; <a href="#B72" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="B72">Hartsel et al., 1983</a>; <a href="#B108" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="B108">Loh et al., 1983</a>; <a href="#B53" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="B53">Fisse and Andres, 1985</a>). Although there are multiple reports on shoot proliferation via micropropagation (<a href="#T3" class="usa-link">Table 3</a>), there are fewer scientific reports showing regeneration of a full plant through <em>de novo</em> regeneration (<a href="#B111" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="B111">Mandolino and Ranalli, 1999</a>; <a href="#B163" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="B163">Slusarkiewicz-Jarzina et al., 2005</a>; <a href="#B194" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="B194">Wielgus et al., 2008</a>; <a href="#B28" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="B28">Chaohua et al., 2016</a>).</p>
<figure class="fig xbox font-sm" id="F3"><h4 class="obj_head">FIGURE 3.</h4>
<p class="img-box line-height-none margin-x-neg-2 tablet:margin-x-0 text-center"><a class="tileshop" target="_blank" href="https://www.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/core/lw/2.0/html/tileshop_pmc/tileshop_pmc_inline.html?title=Click%20on%20image%20to%20zoom&amp;p=PMC3&amp;id=7968383_fpls-12-627240-g003.jpg"><img class="graphic zoom-in" src="https://cdn.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/pmc/blobs/fffd/7968383/5c77d39ae122/fpls-12-627240-g003.jpg" loading="lazy" height="156" width="784" alt="FIGURE 3"></a></p>
<div class="p text-right font-secondary"><a href="figure/F3/" class="usa-link" target="_blank" rel="noopener noreferrer">Open in a new tab</a></div>
