<div class="p text-right font-secondary"><a href="table/T3/" class="usa-link" target="_blank" rel="noopener noreferrer">Open in a new tab</a></div>
<div class="tw-foot p"><div class="fn" id="attrib3"><p><em>The letter ‘m’ indicates the monoecious cultivar. **Indicates studies with the regeneration of shoot or root or both from a single cell.</em></p></div></div></section><p>The majority of regenerated strains and cultivars were monoecious, with few dioecious lines (<a href="#T3" class="usa-link">Table 3</a>). Recently, the optimization of a micropropagation and callogenesis protocol was reported for a few medical cannabis genotypes (<a href="#B133" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="B133">Page et al., 2020</a>). Although 48 years passed (<a href="#F4" class="usa-link">Figure 4</a>) since the first report of <em>in vitro</em> cell culture in cannabis, the available protocols are limited and inconsistent. <em>In vitro</em> regeneration of a cannabis plant from a single cell is still a challenge. Thus, the multi-billion-dollar cannabis industry needs an optimized tissue regeneration protocol for both industrial and medical cannabis.</p>
<figure class="fig xbox font-sm" id="F4"><h4 class="obj_head">FIGURE 4.</h4>
<p class="img-box line-height-none margin-x-neg-2 tablet:margin-x-0 text-center"><a class="tileshop" target="_blank" href="https://www.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/core/lw/2.0/html/tileshop_pmc/tileshop_pmc_inline.html?title=Click%20on%20image%20to%20zoom&amp;p=PMC3&amp;id=7968383_fpls-12-627240-g004.jpg"><img class="graphic zoom-in" src="https://cdn.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/pmc/blobs/fffd/7968383/8dcf9fb1467e/fpls-12-627240-g004.jpg" loading="lazy" height="210" width="758" alt="FIGURE 4"></a></p>
<div class="p text-right font-secondary"><a href="figure/F4/" class="usa-link" target="_blank" rel="noopener noreferrer">Open in a new tab</a></div>
<figcaption><p>Evolution of cannabis tissue culture research. The green curved arrow on the left shows the key events in cannabis use. Each rectangle on the right shows the major research and development activities at different years. Each brown arrow indicates that the technology is continuously developing and research work is in progress in the particular research area.</p></figcaption></figure><p>It is generally understood that the most experienced cannabis companies have developed tissue culture and micropropagation techniques over the last two decades. However, most achievements in this <em>in vitro</em> field are held as a trade secret because of the competitive advantage provided within the industry. The most crucial challenges for the cannabis success micropropagation have been how to (i) reduce the length of subculture to minimize the occupied time and space, (ii) induce better root systems to increase the survival rate to &gt;95%, (iii) optimize Plant Growth Regulators (PGRs), light (intensity and quality) and temperature required to maintain the genetically stable true-to-type clones. A generalized micropropagation workflow would require 7–8 weeks of culture transfer, 3 weeks of shoot multiplication, and 4 weeks of rooting. In terms of PGRs application, the best recommendation is optimized cytokinin and auxin for the vegetative medium and no cytokinin for the rooting medium using full MS media.</p>
<p>In recent years Canadian Licensed producers who are research-oriented have overcome some of these challenges. For example, the acclimatization period has been significantly reduced to less than 3 weeks. Another micropropagation challenge that the cannabis industry has recently solved is optimizing light intensity, light quality, and photoperiod in the culture room and maintaining the most effective temperature during shoot growth and root formation. Some unpublished data shows an increased propagation rate, from 3.5 to 5.8, during sub-culturing from each plantlet, through understanding and obtaining the right abiotic conditions within the culture room. As a starting point, some successful protocols are implemented with the minimum risk of somaclonal variation in cannabis (<a href="#B123" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="B123">Movahedi et al., 2015</a>; <a href="#B102" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="B102">Lata et al., 2016</a>, <a href="#B100" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="B100">2017</a>; <a href="#B69" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="B69">Grulichova et al., 2017</a>; <a href="#B133" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="B133">Page et al., 2020</a>). These are game-changing procedures toward commercialization for cannabis micropropagation at a large-scale operation facility.</p></section><section id="S4.SS2"><h3 class="pmc_sec_title">Genetic Transformation</h3>
<p>An ability to identify, characterize, and apply the genetic variability using biotechnology is the basis of molecular breeding. There are forward and reverse genetics approaches for genetic studies of an uncharacterized allele. With the improvement of sequencing technology, genetic transformation using reverse genetic tools has been an advantage in the molecular breeding program. While cannabis has gained a wide reputation of being recalcitrant to gene transformation and tissue culture, a few reports are describing the methods on gene transformation and regeneration (<a href="#B50" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="B50">Feeney and Punja, 2003</a>; <a href="#B163" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="B163">Slusarkiewicz-Jarzina et al., 2005</a>; <a href="#B161" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="B161">Sirkowski, 2012</a>; <a href="#B185" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="B185">Wahby et al., 2013</a>; <a href="#B154" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="B154">Schachtsiek et al., 2019</a>). Genome editing holds the potential to develop knockout mutants for significant cannabinoid biosynthesis genes such as THCA synthase, CBDA synthase, and CBGA synthase. Several varieties were tested; most were monoecious, although a few dioecious varieties were also used. In all cases, <em>Agrobacterium</em>-mediated gene transfer system was employed and exhibited successful transfer of genes, but the regeneration frequency was low to none. <a href="#B50" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="B50">Feeney and Punja (2003)</a> demonstrated the transformation success at the cellular level, but none of their treatments were successful in regeneration. Similarly, <a href="#B185" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="B185">Wahby et al. (2013)</a> applied <em>A. rhizogenes</em> strains (A4, AR10, C58, and IVIA251) and could induce hairy roots on the explants derived from hypocotyl and cotyledonary node; however, plantlet regeneration became a bottleneck for them as well. There is two patent information with the claim of successful genome modification and regeneration of cannabis with limited descriptions (<a href="#B161" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="B161">Sirkowski, 2012</a>). Thus, there is a need for an optimized protocol for the transformation and regeneration of cannabis replicable and reliable across different species.</p>
<section id="S4.SS2.SSS1"><h4 class="pmc_sec_title">Transient Genetic Transformation</h4>
<p>There are various molecular tools developed for transient genetic transformation, including virus-induced gene silencing (VIGS). VIGSis an RNA mediated post-transcriptional gene silencing (PTGS) technique applied to study gene function in a relatively short period (<a href="#B12" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="B12">Baulcombe, 1999</a>; <a href="#B107" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="B107">Liu et al., 2002</a>; <a href="#B156" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="B156">Senthil-Kumar and Mysore, 2014</a>; <a href="#B3" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="B3">Adhikary et al., 2019</a>). Once a VIGS protocol is established in a species, it takes 3–6 weeks to see the loss-of-function phenotype of the tested gene/s <em>in vivo</em> (<a href="#B3" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="B3">Adhikary et al., 2019</a>). Thus, this is an ideal tool to apply, as a proof of concept, to define a target gene’s function prior to creating a stable transformation. VIGS, using the Cotton leaf crumple virus (CLCrV), was recently established in <em>C. sativa</em>, demonstrating the loss-of-function phenotype of <em>phytoene desaturase</em> (<em>PDS)</em> and <em>magnesium chelatase subunit I</em> (<em>Chll)</em> genes (<a href="#B154" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="B154">Schachtsiek et al., 2019</a>). Although the loss-of-function phenotype was weak, the researchers paved a clear path to explore unknown genes’ functions in the species. There are viral pathogens reported in cannabis (<a href="#B113" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="B113">McPartland, 1996</a>) and many viral vectors developed to date; tobacco rattle virus (TRV) is one of them with a broad-spectrum host range (over 400 plant species) across dicot species (<a href="#B40" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="B40">Dinesh-Kumar et al., 2007</a>). Given that TRV can also infect cannabis, potentially demonstrating a strong loss-of-phenotype than CLCrV viral vector.</p></section><section id="S4.SS2.SSS2"><h4 class="pmc_sec_title">Stable Genetic Transformation</h4>
<p>Both transient and stable transformations have been incredibly beneficial for different research areas and applications in functional genomics. Stable gene transformation is preferred for many applications because once the gene modification is fixed in a plant system, it is heritable. The advantage of the altered gene function can be reaped for generations. As there are numerous reports of successful CRISPR-Cas9 mediated gene editing in many plant species, adopting this newly developed molecular tool in cannabis is vital to improving this economically important plant species. CRISPR can precisely alter a gene’s function in a genome (<a href="#B88" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="B88">Jinek et al., 2012</a>). It has great potential to benefit both basic and applied plant biology research and development. Therefore, establishing the technology in the cannabis crop is essential for functional studies of thousands of unknown genes and the development of novel varieties.</p>
<p>Traditional genetic modification (GM) and gene editing by CRISPR method are viewed differently (<a href="#B159" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="B159">Shew et al., 2018</a>). Gene editing performed using CRISPR method is not considered to be GM organism in some regions. Conventionally, GMO crops refer to organisms that have been altered in a way that they would not have evolved naturally. Moreover, GMO involves transferring foreign DNA fragment from one species to another (transgenic) or within the same species (cisgenic). But in the case of CRISPR edited plants, the targeted mutation is created by using an enzyme and a small guide RNA. While the mutation continues to inherited, the CRISPR machinery can be eliminated in the next generation (<a href="#B6" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="B6">Aliaga-Franco et al., 2019</a>). This method is precise and faster than conventional breeding practices, and it is much less controversial than GMO techniques. Therefore, the establishment of CRISPR-Cas9 system in cannabis is another crucial aspect that needs to be explored.</p></section></section><section id="S4.SS3"><h3 class="pmc_sec_title">Hairy Root Culture</h3>
<p><em>Agrobacterium rhizogenes</em> is another functional genomics tool to assess the function of a gene or developing transgenic plants. These are differentiated cultures that are induced by the infection of <em>Agrobacterium rhizogenes</em>, a soil bacterium. Hairy root culture has a high growth rate in a hormone-free medium and exhibits the potential to yield secondary metabolites comparable to the wildtype (<a href="#B138" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="B138">Pistelli et al., 2010</a>). It enables the use of stable and reproducible bioreactor-based production and extraction independent of weather conditions, regulatory hurdles, and a lower risk of microbial contamination. This is a safe approach for producing medicinal and active metabolites free of hormones/viruses and does not require pesticides or insecticides. It is also one of the critical avenues for cannabis genetic transformation and functional genomics research.</p>
<p>Calli or hypocotyls infected by <em>A. rhizogenes</em> respond with the emergence of hairy roots from the infected site. Hairy roots can be individually selected and tested for a higher production rate of a compound of interest and cryopreserved at –196°C as a pure culture and subculture further for commercial-scale production (<a href="#B44" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="B44">Engelmann, 2004</a>). Cannabis hairy root culture has been successfully induced by <em>A. rhizogenes</em> (<a href="#B184" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="B184">Wahby et al., 2006</a>, <a href="#B185" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="B185">2013</a>). Hairy root cultures from cannabis callus were also reported using 4 mg/l NAA as a supplement to B5 medium under dark conditions at 25°C (<a href="#B47" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="B47">Farag and Kayser, 2015</a>). In the study, the level of THCA and CBDA was less than 2 μg/g dry weight indicating a very low level of cannabinoids present in the hairy root culture under the dark condition with a 28-day growth cycle.</p>
<p>While detectable levels of cannabinoids are not present in <em>C. sativa</em> hairy roots, they have been reported to contain choline, atropine, and muscarine (<a href="#B184" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="B184">Wahby et al., 2006</a>, <a href="#B186" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="B186">2017</a>). A higher level of these compounds was observed in the <em>A. rhizogenes</em> transformed hairy roots compared to non-transformed control. Choline was the most significant compound ranged between 203 and 510 mg/L (control 66–153 mg/L); Atropine with 562–933 μg/L (control 532–553 μg/L); Muscarine with 231–367 μg/L (control undetectable) (<a href="#B186" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="B186">Wahby et al., 2017</a>). Additionally, the THCA synthase gene’s heterologous expression in tobacco hairy root culture has been successful (<a href="#B160" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="B160">Sirikantaramas et al., 2004</a>; <a href="#B179" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="B179">Taura et al., 2009</a>).</p></section><section id="S4.SS4"><h3 class="pmc_sec_title">Meristem Culture</h3>
<p>The culture of indeterminate organs, especially the totipotent cells in the apical dome, is a method to obtain many virus clones in a short period (<a href="#B121" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="B121">Mori, 1971</a>; <a href="#B188" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="B188">Wang and Charles, 1991</a>). The apical dome region has no vascular connection to the developing procambium, leaf primordium, and axillary buds (<a href="#B188" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="B188">Wang and Charles, 1991</a>). This lack of vascular connection provides a basis for using the meristem for pathogen elimination as viruses readily travel through the vascular system but do not efficiently transfer from cell to cell. Uninfected cells can be isolated from the meristematic dome (<a href="#B188" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="B188">Wang and Charles, 1991</a>; <a href="#B196" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="B196">Wu et al., 2020</a>). It is a robust tool for producingvirus-free clones that can then be further multiplied at a commercial scale to produce certified virus-free plants. Characteristically, a section of tissue, mostly the apical dome, is dissected either from apical or lateral buds consisting of leaf primordia (no more than 1–2 in number) and apical meristem (0.1–0.5 mm in length) and cultured in a suitable growth medium. Upon induction of the meristem cells under a favorable combination of hormones and growth environment, the cells can continue to develop into a shoot or regenerate into plants through somatic embryogenesis or shoot organogenesis. The regeneration process occasionally gives direct shoot development from the explant, and sometimes morphogenesis occurs indirectly only after the formation of the callus.</p>
<p>There are well-established meristem culture protocols for different model and non-model species (<a href="#B121" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="B121">Mori, 1971</a>; <a href="#B120" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="B120">Mordhorst et al., 2002</a>; <a href="#B8" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="B8">Al-Taleb et al., 2011</a>; <a href="#B172" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="B172">Spanò et al., 2018</a>), including the closest relative of cannabis, <em>Humulus lupulus</em> (Hops), for eliminating virus infection (<a href="#B67" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="B67">Grudzinska and Solarska, 2004</a>; <a href="#B68" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="B68">Grudzinska et al., 2006</a>; <a href="#B2" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="B2">Adams, 2015</a>; <a href="#B151" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="B151">Sallie and Jones, 2015</a>). Given the importance of cannabis as a crop, the development of meristem culture for clean plant production could be useful. Unfortunately, this technique is most effective with viral diseases and would not eliminate fungal and bacterial pathogens known to infect cannabis.</p></section><section id="S4.SS5"><h3 class="pmc_sec_title">Protoplast Culture</h3>
<p>For decades, plant protoplasts have been used for genetic transformation, cell fusion, somatic mutation, and more recently, for genome editing (<a href="#B104" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="B104">Lei et al., 2015</a>). Significant progress has been made in other crop species in genetic studies using protoplasts; however, for cannabis, studies are in a development phase, with the conditions suitable for the survival of transfected protoplasts and plant regeneration are yet to be optimized. Mesophyll protoplast isolation and transformation of at least three different cannabis cultivars has been reported (<a href="#B122" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="B122">Morimoto et al., 2007</a>; <a href="#B55" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="B55">Flaishman et al., 2019</a>). Based on the recent study, only about 4% of the protoplasts survived 48 h in liquid culture and plants were not regenerated (<a href="#B55" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="B55">Flaishman et al., 2019</a>). Even in the absence of successful regeneration of a whole plant, protoplasts are of great value in confirming the effectiveness of designed guide RNA (gRNA) prior to their use for the regeneration of gene-edited plants.</p></section><section id="S4.SS6"><h3 class="pmc_sec_title">Somatic Embryogenesis</h3>
<p>Somatic embryogenesis is the regeneration of a whole plant from cultured plant cells via embryo formation, from somatic plant cells of various tissues like root, stem, leaf, hypocotyl, cotyledon or petiole (<a href="#B158" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="B158">Shen et al., 2018</a>). They morphologically resemble the zygotic embryo’s bipolar structure, bear specific embryonic organs, and go through analogous development stages with similar gene expression profiles (<a href="#B158" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="B158">Shen et al., 2018</a>). Somatic embryogenesis can occur through direct regeneration. The embryos are developed directly from explant cells, or more commonly through indirect regeneration in which callus develops first, and the development of embryos occurs from callus cells (<a href="#B157" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="B157">Sharp et al., 1980</a>).</p>
<p>Plant regeneration via somatic embryogenesis starts with the initiation of embryogenic cultures by culturing various explants on media supplemented with only auxins or a combination of auxins and cytokinins to control cell growth and development (<a href="#B132" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="B132">Osborne and McManus, 2005</a>). One exception to this is the use of thidiazuron (TDZ), a cytokinin-like compound that is often used alone to induce somatic embryogenesis (<a href="#B125" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="B125">Murthy et al., 1995</a>). The proliferation of embryogenic cultures can occur on solid or in liquid media supplemented with auxins and cytokinins, followed by pre-maturation of somatic embryos on lower levels of PGRs or PGR free media to stimulate somatic embryo formation and development. Maturation of somatic embryos can occur by culturing on media with reduced osmotic potential or supplemented with abscisic acid (<a href="#B62" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="B62">George et al., 2007</a>). This maturation stage is critical for synthetic seed production as it allows embryos to be desiccated, stored, encapsulated, and treated like regular seeds. However, in many somatic embryogenesis systems, the maturation phase has not been developed, and somatic embryos germinate precociously to produce plants.</p>
<p>Somatic embryos are used as a model system in embryology studies; however, somatic embryogenesis’s main economic applications are for developing transgenic plants and large-scale virus-free vegetative propagation of elite plant genotypes. The possibility to scale up the propagation using bioreactors has been reported (<a href="#B81" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="B81">Hvoslef-Eide and Preil, 2005</a>). Somatic embryos are also ideal for genetic manipulation purposes as they develop from a single cell, thereby reducing the chances of producing chimeric plants, common when relying on shoot organogenesis or shoot proliferation (<a href="#B39" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="B39">Dhekney et al., 2016</a>). Other less common uses of somatic embryogenesis include cryopreservation of genetic materials and synthetic seed technology (<a href="#B62" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="B62">George et al., 2007</a>).</p>
<p><a href="#B50" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="B50">Feeney and Punja (2003)</a> investigated the somatic embryogenesis and tissue culture propagation of hemp. Despite testing various explants and supplements, and variations in the culture medium and changes to the culture environment, there was no successful plantlet regeneration, and a reliable protocol for somatic embryogenesis in cannabis has yet to be published.</p></section><section id="S4.SS7"><h3 class="pmc_sec_title">Thin Cell Layer (TCL)</h3>
<p>Thin cell layer (TCL) culture utilizes a thin layer of tissue as the explant to allow close contact between wounded cells and nutrients and growth regulators supplied in the medium; this controls the morphogenesis of the cultures (<a href="#B128" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="B128">Nhut et al., 2003</a>). This is most useful where larger explants may also contain a high level of endogenous hormones, carbon sources, and other substances that influence and conflict with the effects of exogenous substances placed in the medium and, thus, interfere with development. In general, sterilized TCL explants are excised either longitudinally (0.5–1 mm wide, 5–10 mm long) or transversally (0.1–5 mm thick) prior to culturing (<a href="#B128" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="B128">Nhut et al., 2003</a>; <a href="#B36" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="B36">Croom et al., 2016</a>). Like other <em>in vitro</em> techniques, TCL requires an optimized protocol regarding basal media, PGRs and other added nutrients and growth conditions such as daylength, light intensity, and temperature. These conditions vary for not only the species but can be genotype-dependent. It has been widely used in different species, including bamboo, banana, citrus, tomato, rose, <em>Lilium ledebourii</em>, <em>Bacopa monnieri</em>, saffron, among others (<a href="#B128" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="B128">Nhut et al., 2003</a>; <a href="#B180" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="B180">Teixeira da Silva et al., 2007</a>; <a href="#B119" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="B119">Mirmasoumi et al., 2013</a>; <a href="#B36" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="B36">Croom et al., 2016</a>; <a href="#B10" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="B10">Azadi et al., 2017</a>). TCL’s potential is yet to be explored in <em>Cannabis</em> spp.; however, it may prove to have some utility in the regeneration of genetic transformants in this high value but re calcitrant regeneration crop.</p></section><section id="S4.SS8"><h3 class="pmc_sec_title">Doubled Haploid Production</h3>
<p>Androgenesis is a biological process by which a whole plant regenerates directly from immature pollen (microspores) through the embryogenesis developmental pathway under <em>in vitro</em> conditions. While the resulting plant is haploid and inherently sterile, a diploid plant can arise either spontaneously or artificially (<a href="#B63" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="B63">Gilles et al., 2017</a>), usually with colchicine, which blocks cytokinesis without blocking chromosome doubling (<a href="#B60" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="B60">Galazkajoa and Niemirowicz-Szczytt, 2013</a>). This doubled haploid is homozygous at all loci. Doubled Haploid (DH) plants have been extensively used in plant breeding programs to increase the speed and efficiency with which homozygous lines can be obtained (<a href="#B57" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="B57">Alisher et al., 2007</a>). DH technology is traditionally used to genetically stabilize parental lines for F<sub>1</sub> hybrid production. This is important for the rapid integration of new traits through backcross conversion and to develop molecular mapping populations. It is also used to fix desired traits obtained through transformation or mutagenesis and simplify genomic sequencing by eliminating heterozygosity (<a href="#B52" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="B52">Ferrie and Mollers, 2011</a>). As such, this technology would be an important tool for both forward and reverse functional genomics studies.</p>
<p>There are two different approaches to develop haploid plants. First, <em>in situ</em> methods, using particular pollination techniques such as irradiated pollen, inter-species crosses or so-called ‘inducer lines’ (<a href="#B145" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="B145">Ren et al., 2017</a>); second, <em>in vitro</em> methods including the culture of haploid cells (gametes) and their development to haploid embryos and consequently haploid plants through germination. The microspores, which can be harvested in large numbers (millions), are generally isolated for culture as a uniform population. Alternatively, the culture of whole anthers is used to obtain haploid plants through the androgenesis process. The main disadvantage of another culture is the potential for developing a mix of both haploid and diploid plantlets (<a href="#B43" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="B43">Elhiti et al., 2010</a>). In this review, we will focus only on the production of doubled haploids from microspores using <em>in vitro</em> culture.</p>
<p>One of the most important factors affecting DH production is the microspore developmental stage. It is a complicated factor that has a strong influence on microspore culture’s success. It has been reported that only microspores that are at a stage sufficiently immature have the ability to change their developmental fate from a gametophytic to embryogenic, leading to sporophytic development (<a href="#B171" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="B171">Soriano et al., 2013</a>). The most amenable stage is either the uni-nucleate stage of the microspore or the early binucleate stage, either at or just after the first pollen mitosis. At this developmental stage, the microspore’s transcriptional status may still be proliferative and not yet fully differentiated (<a href="#B110" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="B110">Malik et al., 2007</a>). Although all microspores within an anther would be roughly of a similar age, not all cells have embryonic competence. Therefore, the incremental differences in the stages of development of individual microspores can be considered significant. To avoid this problem, <a href="#B14" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="B14">Bhowmik et al. (2011)</a> introduced a new treatment, discontinuous Percoll gradient centrifugation, to provide a uniform population of <em>B. napus</em> isolated microspores at the appropriate stage of development. This approach has consistently produced high embryo yields and consistent embryo development.</p>
<section id="S4.SS8.SSS1"><h4 class="pmc_sec_title">Hemp Microspore Culture</h4>
<p>In 2019, an extensive hemp breeding program was introduced at Haplotech Inc.<sup><a href="#footnote1" class="usa-link">1</a></sup>. As there has been no previously reported success in the area, a hemp DH project was initiated to accelerate this program. Four different Haplotech genotypes were used for this experiment. Both male racemes and pollen-induced female colas were collected, and the buds were fractionated according to size into three groups (2–3, 3–4, and 4–5 mm). Each group was surface sterilized with 15% commercial bleach and washed three times with distilled-sterilized water for 5 min each. The sterilized buds were macerated in isolation media (MS basal fortified by 13% sucrose). The isolated microspores were washed by extraction medium two times or until the supernatant became clear. The isolated microspores were subjected to fractional centrifugation using Percoll, as described by <a href="#B14" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="B14">Bhowmik et al. (2011)</a>. The concentration of microspores was diluted to 4 × 10<sup>4</sup>cells/ml with MS basal fortified by 10% sucrose. Five ml of this diluent (4 × 10<sup>4</sup>) microspores were mixed with 5 ml of induction media (MS basal, 10% sucrose supplemented with different additives for induction) in 47 mm Petri dishes. The final concentration of the culture used was 2 × 10<sup>4</sup> cells/ml. The isolated microspores in culture were observed every 3 days using an inverted microscope and a binocular microscope.</p>
<p>Samples of isolated microspores were stained with 4, 6-diamidino-2-phenylindole (DAPI) and observed using a fluorescence microscope to monitor their <em>in vitro</em> development, once every 3 days. Monitoring of the culture samples by DAPI staining in the first 2 weeks revealed that the microspores of all four genotypes remained uninuclear (<a href="#F5" class="usa-link">Figure 5A</a>). This developmental stage was found to be the most responsive to embryogenesis induction in many crop plants (<a href="#B171" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="B171">Soriano et al., 2013</a>). Of the factors tested, the most crucial for further development of the microspore was the induction medium formulation. Using a relatively complex medium, a few microspores responded (0.05–0.5%) and developed further, while the remainder died within 5–10 days. Microspore derived embryos initiated by a series of random divisions within the surrounding exine wall. The nucleus of uninucleate microspores (<a href="#F5" class="usa-link">Figure 5A</a>) condensed and reduced in size during the first 2 days in culture (<a href="#F5" class="usa-link">Figure 5B</a>). They then divided symmetrically within the first 5–8 days, forming two equal-sized nuclei (<a href="#F5" class="usa-link">Figure 5C</a>). This developmental stage is considered the initial stage that is often referred to as sporophytic growth (<a href="#B171" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="B171">Soriano et al., 2013</a>). Within another 3–5 days, the nuclei underwent a series of divisions resulting in the formation of multinucleate structures (<a href="#F5" class="usa-link">Figure 5D</a>). By approximately the third week of culture, globular stage embryos were observed in culture (<a href="#F5" class="usa-link">Figure 5E</a>). Early in the fourth week, these globular structures developed into heart stage embryos (<a href="#F5" class="usa-link">Figure 5F</a>). To date, growth has not progressed past this stage of embryo development. Current experiments including adjustment of the osmoticum and removal of secondary metabolites which could inhibit (microspore-derived) embryo development are running.</p>
<figure class="fig xbox font-sm" id="F5"><h5 class="obj_head">FIGURE 5.</h5>
<p class="img-box line-height-none margin-x-neg-2 tablet:margin-x-0 text-center"><a class="tileshop" target="_blank" href="https://www.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/core/lw/2.0/html/tileshop_pmc/tileshop_pmc_inline.html?title=Click%20on%20image%20to%20zoom&amp;p=PMC3&amp;id=7968383_fpls-12-627240-g005.jpg"><img class="graphic zoom-in" src="https://cdn.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/pmc/blobs/fffd/7968383/320bfbb4f8c3/fpls-12-627240-g005.jpg" loading="lazy" height="462" width="800" alt="FIGURE 5"></a></p>
<div class="p text-right font-secondary"><a href="figure/F5/" class="usa-link" target="_blank" rel="noopener noreferrer">Open in a new tab</a></div>
<figcaption><p>Developmental pathways observed in <em>C. sativa</em> (industrial hemp) microspore culture. <strong>(A–C)</strong> Male gametophyte development in <em>C. sativa</em> during <em>in vitro</em> culture. <strong>(A)</strong> Uninucleate microspores; <strong>(B)</strong> uninucleate microspores after 3 days in culture media; <strong>(C)</strong> symmetrically divided microspore with two equally sized nuclei; <strong>(D)</strong> multinucleate structure without organization and still enclosed in exine; <strong>(E)</strong> globular multicellular structure with developing exine; and <strong>(F)</strong> heart-shape embryo with two distinct domains. The nuclei in <strong>(A–C)</strong> are stained with the nuclear dye 4′,6-diamidino-2-phenylindole (DAPI) to indicate viability.</p></figcaption></figure></section><section id="S4.SS8.SSS2"><h4 class="pmc_sec_title">
<em>In vitro</em> Mutagenesis</h4>
<p>A mutation occurs in DNA, naturally or it can also be induced artificially. The majority of the genetic variation existing in a gene pool has occurred naturally. These genetic variations can be recombined through conventional breeding practices to develop a novel variety with desired gene traits. Although these spontaneous mutations are frequent, the desired mutation in the desired gene segment altering its biological role is extremely rare. Therefore, mutation induction tools are used in the rapid development of genetic variability in crops. For the last few decades, there were several scientific reports published assessing the impact of an induced mutation in the improvement of crops (<a href="#B17" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="B17">Brock, 1971</a>; <a href="#B18" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="B18">Broertjes and Van Harten, 1988</a>; <a href="#B117" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="B117">Micke, 1999</a>; <a href="#B131" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="B131">Oladosu et al., 2016</a>). However, in cannabis research and development is rapidly flourishing, but there are only a few reports on targeted mutation through genetic transformation (<a href="#B50" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="B50">Feeney and Punja, 2003</a>; <a href="#B163" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="B163">Slusarkiewicz-Jarzina et al., 2005</a>; <a href="#B161" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="B161">Sirkowski, 2012</a>; <a href="#B185" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="B185">Wahby et al., 2013</a>) and there is no mutant variety introduced at the commercial level. <em>In vitro</em> culture techniques, coupled with mutagenesis, has simplified the crop improvement work for both seeds and vegetatively propagated plants (<a href="#B79" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="B79">Hussain et al., 2012</a>). Little efforts have been made and published to establish DH production in cannabis, but once streamlined will open up exciting opportunities for DH mutagenesis as it has been successfully employed in canola (<a href="#B175" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="B175">Szarejko, 2003</a>).</p></section></section><section id="S4.SS9"><h3 class="pmc_sec_title">Synthetic Seed Technology</h3>
<p>Synthetic seeds usually refer to artificially encapsulated somatic embryos (<a href="#B124" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="B124">Murashige, 1977</a>) but have also been used in reference to encapsulated vegetative tissues that have the potential to develop into a whole plant (auxiliary buds, cell aggregates, shoot buds). Somatic embryos provide the ideal approach to developing synthetic seeds as they often have the ability to survive desiccation and can be treated in much the same way as true seeds. At the same time, other tissues lack this capacity and are less useful (<a href="#B147" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="B147">Rihan et al., 2017</a>). As shown in <a href="#F6" class="usa-link">Figure 6</a>, synthetic seeds can be successfully developed by using various explants, media, and encapsulation protocols (<a href="#B11" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="B11">Bapat et al., 1987</a>; <a href="#B35" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="B35">Corrie and Tandon, 1993</a>; <a href="#B130" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="B130">Nyende et al., 2003</a>; <a href="#B24" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="B24">Chand and Singh, 2004</a>; <a href="#B143" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="B143">Rai et al., 2008</a>; <a href="#B96" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="B96">Lata et al., 2009a</a>).</p>
<figure class="fig xbox font-sm" id="F6"><h4 class="obj_head">FIGURE 6.</h4>
<p class="img-box line-height-none margin-x-neg-2 tablet:margin-x-0 text-center"><a class="tileshop" target="_blank" href="https://www.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/core/lw/2.0/html/tileshop_pmc/tileshop_pmc_inline.html?title=Click%20on%20image%20to%20zoom&amp;p=PMC3&amp;id=7968383_fpls-12-627240-g006.jpg"><img class="graphic zoom-in" src="https://cdn.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/pmc/blobs/fffd/7968383/ec00f2fb68a2/fpls-12-627240-g006.jpg" loading="lazy" height="292" width="784" alt="FIGURE 6"></a></p>
<div class="p text-right font-secondary"><a href="figure/F6/" class="usa-link" target="_blank" rel="noopener noreferrer">Open in a new tab</a></div>
<figcaption><p>General schematic diagram showing steps for calcium chloride encapsulated synthetic seed production.</p></figcaption></figure><p>Cannabis is generally a cross-pollinating crop, and due to its allogamous nature, it is difficult to maintain existing elite varieties by seed. Typically, a minimum isolation distance of 5 km between breeding nurseries and hemp production fields is required to minimize the occurrence of nuisance pollen. Such separation is often difficult to achieve in areas with high hemp production intensity. Therefore, <em>in vitro</em> propagation using synthetic seed technology is an alternative method for large-scale clonal propagation and germplasm preservation. As the cannabis industry grows, this method may be cheaper and faster than traditional tissue culture methods. Along with the preservation of genetic uniformity, clones produced through this technique are pathogen-free, easy to handle, and transport.</p>
<p>Moreover, in other species, this approach has resulted in increased quality of planting material (<a href="#B147" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="B147">Rihan et al., 2017</a>). While cannabis tissue culture methods are still being optimized, <a href="#B96" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="B96">Lata et al. (2009a)</a> developed a high-frequency propagation of axillary buds of <em>C. sativa</em> encapsulated in calcium alginate gel. Calcium alginate is a hydrogel that contains nutrients, growth regulators, and sometimes antibiotics.</p>
<p>When directly sown on a substrate, encapsulation aids in the physical protection and establishment and growth of the explant. According to <a href="#B96" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="B96">Lata et al. (2009a)</a>, gel capsule consisted of 5% sodium alginate with 50 mM CaCl<sub>2</sub>.2H<sub>2</sub>O, and full-strength MS medium supplied with 0.5 μM TDZ, and 0.075% plant preservative mixture (PPM). The optimal regrowth and conversion were achieved in MS medium supplemented with antimicrobial components, PPM (0.075%) and TDZ (0.5 μM) under <em>in vitro</em> conditions. Under <em>in vivo</em> condition, the optimal conversion and regrowth were exhibited on 1:1 potting mix-fertilome with coco natural growth medium supplied with MS medium containing 3% sucrose, 0.5% PPM. Clones regenerated from the explants were successfully hardened and transferred to the soil (<a href="#B96" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="B96">Lata et al., 2009a</a>).</p>
<p>Another hurdle to <em>in vitro</em> propagation is transporting requested strains from the tissue culture facility to the growers in a timely manner. These transportation issues become incredibly challenging for maintaining crop schedules because cannabis crops can take more than 2 months to reach hardening stages, then spend 4 weeks in vegetative growth, then 7 or 8 weeks in flower. Greenhouse or indoor growers require a consistent supply demand to receive a high volume of plantlets every week to start over a new grow room at a very tight on-time delivery schedule, which is the most important metric in their operations. An established and cost-effective synthetic seed encapsulation technique would provide an opportunity to eliminate the transportation challenge.</p></section></section><section id="S5"><h2 class="pmc_sec_title">Cryopreservation</h2>
<p>Cryopreservation refers to the storage of diverse living materials at below –130°C (<a href="#B44" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="B44">Engelmann, 2004</a>). It serves as an alternative conservation approach to the conventional field and <em>in vitro</em> (i.e., slow growth) germplasm conservation and is cost-effective over extended periods with minimal space and routine maintenance requirements (<a href="#B135" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="B135">Pence, 2011</a>; <a href="#B45" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="B45">Engelmann, 2014</a>; <a href="#B141" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="B141">Popova et al., 2015</a>). It also assists current breeding programs by providing long-term storage and an easy long-distance exchange of genetic materials (e.g., pollen and meristematic apices and buds). Cryopreservation has been implemented for various plant species using different methods, the most popular and widely applicable, including controlled freezing, vitrification, encapsulation-dehydration, encapsulation-vitrification, and droplet-vitrification (<a href="#B150" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="B150">Sakai and Engelmann, 2007</a>; <a href="#B141" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="B141">Popova et al., 2015</a>). These methods follow distinct approaches to dehydrate cryopreserving living materials by converting liquid water to a glassy state to avoid the lethal formation of intracellular ice. The selection of methods and the scales of conservation using this approach are strongly determined by genotypes and tissue materials used, which contain different responses to pre- and post-cryopreservation treatments.</p>
<p>Conventional and <em>in vitro</em> conservation of cannabis require considerable amounts of space and routine maintenance, have genetic mutations accumulate in the plants. Conventional conservation may expose plants to virulence pathogens. The plants may eventually become susceptible to diseases. The application of cryopreservation can serve as an essential tool for the conservation of various valuable <em>C. sativa</em> genotypes with unique attributes and trading the genotypes nationally and internationally in sterile conditions. The first study on applying cryopreservation techniques in <em>C. sativa</em> was reported in 1989 using cell suspension cultures (<a href="#B84" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="B84">Jekkel et al., 1989</a>). The suspension cultures were preserved using 10% dimethyl sulfoxide (DMSO) cryoprotectant and a controlled cooling rate of 2°C/min and transfer temperature of –10°C, with a 58% survival rate after cryopreservation of the cultures. A cryopreservation protocol for <em>C. sativa</em> shoot tips was recently developed using a droplet-vitrification in liquid nitrogen for long-term conservation of this crop (<a href="#B182" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="B182">Uchendu et al., 2019</a>). The report showed that vitrified shoot tips using a cryoprotectant solution of 30% glycerol, 15% ethylene glycol, 15% DMSO in liquid MS medium with 0.4 M sucrose, pH 5.8 had 63% re-growth efficiency. Despite the promising progress made, more studies need to be done on selecting appropriate cryopreservation methods with respect to the tissue types and genotypes, increasing re-growth and survival efficiency of preserved samples, and genetic stability of regenerated plants after using different cryopreservation tools, among others.</p></section><section id="S6"><h2 class="pmc_sec_title">Germplasm Maintenance</h2>
<p>The <em>in vitro</em> condition also raises some issues for concern, primarily when the material is maintained over a long period of time.</p>
<section id="S6.SS1"><h3 class="pmc_sec_title">Clonal Stability <em>in vitro</em> Culture</h3>
<p><em>In vitro</em> mass-propagation and maintenance of elite germplasm requires genetically stable true-to-type clones. Several factors, such as the number of subcultures, changes in the relationship of auxin/cytokinin, explant type, and a high concentration of growth regulators, may influence the genetic stability of a clone under <em>in vitro</em> conditions (<a href="#B89" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="B89">Joyce et al., 2003</a>; <a href="#B152" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="B152">Sato et al., 2011</a>; <a href="#B169" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="B169">Smulders and de Klerk, 2011</a>; <a href="#B129" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="B129">Nwauzoma and Jaja, 2013</a>). While carefully selecting explant types and optimizing the conditions above, but depending on the plant species, clonal stability can be obtained during <em>in vitro</em> mass-propagation and germplasm conservation of the desired elite genotypes maintained. To date, <em>C. sativa</em> plants regenerated from nodal culture, and <em>in vitro</em> conserved synthetic seeds (‘Encapsulated’ nodal segments) have shown no evidence of genetic mutations; however, this has only been evaluated using low numbers of markers (<a href="#B98" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="B98">Lata et al., 2010a</a>, <a href="#B101" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="B101">2011</a>). Despite optimizing and using properly <em>in vitro</em> conditions that limit somaclonal variations, assessment of clonal stability is required to ensure the regenerated clones are the true-to-type of the donor plants.</p></section><section id="S6.SS2"><h3 class="pmc_sec_title">Somaclonal Variation</h3>
<p>Although clonal propagation and maintenance of elite germplasm require a substantial genetic uniformity among <em>in vitro</em> regenerated plantlets, there may be a large possibility of genetic variations, called “somaclonal variation” among these plants and/or relative to the donor plants. Somaclonal variation is commonly a result of genetic alterations and changes in the new <em>in vitro</em> plants’ epigenetics compared to the original source plants (<a href="#B118" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="B118">Miguel and Marum, 2011</a>; <a href="#B1" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="B1">Abreu et al., 2014</a>). The frequency and nature of somaclonal variation <em>in vitro</em> culture can be influenced by different factors, such as explant source, genotype, <em>in vitro</em> techniques, <em>in vitro</em> growth conditions, length of the culture period, and the number of subcultures. The use of <em>de novo</em> regeneration from highly differentiated tissues (i.e., roots, leaves, stems, hypocotyls, cotyledons, etc.) is generally considered to produce more somaclonal variation compared to explants with developed meristems (i.e., axillary buds and shoot tips) (<a href="#B136" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="B136">Pijut et al., 2012</a>). Most of these factors generate oxidative stress during culture initiation and subsequent subculturing. The explants and the subsequent regenerated plants exposed to the stress may retain genetic changes. For example, protoplast and callus based plant regeneration impose a high degree of oxidative stress; thus, the stress promotes a high mutation rate, whereas plants regenerated through auxiliary branching (e.g., nodes, shoot tip) experience very low oxidative stress, normally resulting in no genetic variation (<a href="#B197" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="B197">Zayova et al., 2010</a>; <a href="#B169" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="B169">Smulders and de Klerk, 2011</a>; <a href="#B94" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="B94">Krishna et al., 2016</a>). Genetic variation can also arise from somatic mutations already present in the explants collected from the donor plant (<a href="#B90" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="B90">Karp, 1994</a>). <em>In vitro</em> regeneration of plants can also be genotype-specific, in which genotypes have different degrees of mutation risks and thus strongly determine the formation of somaclonal variation (<a href="#B7" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="B7">Alizadeh et al., 2010</a>; <a href="#B42" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="B42">Eftekhari et al., 2012</a>; <a href="#B129" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="B129">Nwauzoma and Jaja, 2013</a>). The genetic alterations strongly depend on the <em>in vitro</em> techniques used to regenerate <em>in vitro</em> plants. Additionally, despite differences across plant species, cultures maintained for a long period tend to generate high somaclonal variation, and <em>vice versa</em> (<a href="#B49" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="B49">Farahani et al., 2011</a>; <a href="#B85" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="B85">Jevremovic et al., 2012</a>; <a href="#B174" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="B174">Sun et al., 2013</a>). When cultures are getting old and continuously subcultured, the chance of generating genetically less uniform plants is increased (<a href="#B197" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="B197">Zayova et al., 2010</a>), but depends upon the plant species. For example, any more than eight subculture cycles increased somaclonal variation in banana (<a href="#B91" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="B91">Khan et al., 2011</a>), whereas over 30 subcultures did not cause any detectable somaclonal variations in <em>C. sativa</em> (<a href="#B98" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="B98">Lata et al., 2010a</a>).</p>
<p>Although the molecular mechanism of how somaclonal variations generated from a single plant genotype under the same <em>in vitro</em> conditions is not fully explored, several potential mechanisms causing genetic alternations and epigenetics have been proposed in different plant species. These mechanisms include changes in chromosome number, point mutations, somatic crossing over and sister chromatid exchange, chromosome breakage and rearrangement, somatic gene rearrangement, DNA replication, changes in organelle DNA, insertion or excision of transposable elements, segregation of pre-existing chimeral tissues, DNA methylation, epigenetic variation, and histone modifications and RNA interference (<a href="#B152" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="B152">Sato et al., 2011</a>; <a href="#B94" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="B94">Krishna et al., 2016</a>; references therein).</p>
<p>The occurrence of somaclonal variations in regenerated <em>in vitro</em> plants may be advantageous or disadvantageous, depending on <em>in vitro</em> propagation goals. If <em>in vitro</em> propagation aims to generate new variants, obtaining variations among <em>in vitro</em> plants can be advantageous that increases genetic diversity for a genotype used. It provides an alternative tool to the breeders for obtaining genetic variability in different plant species, which are either difficult to breed or have narrow genetic bases. On the flip side, when <em>in vitro</em> propagation targets to produce multiple true-to-type <em>in vitro</em> plants and maintain elite germplasm, the occurrence of subtle somaclonal variations is a severe problem.</p></section></section><section id="S7"><h2 class="pmc_sec_title">Phytocannabinoid Synthesis in the Cannabis Species</h2>
<p>Nature has deftly adorned cannabis species with a spectrum of phytocannabinoids or monoterpenoids that are chemically designed with para-oriented isoprenyl and aralkyl groups (<a href="#B71" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="B71">Hanus et al., 2016</a>). Since the discovery of tetrahydrocannabinol (THC) and cannabidiol (CBD) in the early 1960s, there are over 120 cannabinoids that has been reported, and the biosynthesis pathway of these compounds has been greatly improved (<a href="#B176" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="B176">Taura et al., 1995</a>; <a href="#B160" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="B160">Sirikantaramas et al., 2004</a>; <a href="#B178" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="B178">Taura et al., 2007b</a>, <a href="#B179" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="B179">2009</a>; <a href="#B58" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="B58">Gagne et al., 2012</a>; <a href="#B173" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="B173">Stout et al., 2012</a>; <a href="#B103" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="B103">Laverty et al., 2019</a>). Presumably, cannabigerolic acid (CBGA), the product formed by the alkylation of geranyl diphosphate and olivetol, is the key precursor compound in the synthesis of cannabinoids (<a href="#B51" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="B51">Fellermeier and Zenk, 1998</a>). The cyclization event of prenyl components of CBGA, catalyzed by three enzymes – tetrahydrocannabinolic acid synthase (THCAS) (genebank accession: <a href="https://ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/nucleotide/AB057805" class="usa-link" data-ga-action="click_feat_suppl" target="_blank" rel="noopener noreferrer">AB057805</a>), cannabidiolic acid synthase (CBDAS) (genebank accession: <a href="https://ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/nucleotide/AB292682" class="usa-link" data-ga-action="click_feat_suppl" target="_blank" rel="noopener noreferrer">AB292682</a>), and cannabichromenic acid synthase (CBCAS), lead to the formation of three major cannabinoids, THCA, CBDA, and CBCA, respectively (<a href="#B160" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="B160">Sirikantaramas et al., 2004</a>; <a href="#B177" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="B177">Taura et al., 2007a</a>). Biochemical characterization of the enzymes, THCAS and CBDAS, have demonstrated that the enzymes follow a similar reaction mechanism. In the presence of molecular oxygen, the enzymes use flavin adenine dinucleotide (FAD) cofactor to catalyze CBGA forming THCA and CBDA, and hydrogen peroxide as its chemical biproduct (<a href="#B160" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="B160">Sirikantaramas et al., 2004</a>; <a href="#B178" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="B178">Taura et al., 2007b</a>). Although it is a bit unclear, the chemical reaction for CBCAS also believed to use FAD as cofactor and molecular oxygen to complete the enzymatic activity on CBGA. The genes that encode for CBCAS and THCAS are highly similar in the nucleotide level, indicating that CBCAS is also flavoproteins, like the other two enzymes, requiring oxygen to catalyze CBGA to CBCA (<a href="#B103" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="B103">Laverty et al., 2019</a>). THCA, CBDA, and CBCA are the major cannabinoids in acidic forms that are synthesized in cannabis plant; upon decarboxylation, these compounds convert into neutral forms, THC, CBD, and CBC respectively (<a href="#B187" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="B187">Wang et al., 2016</a>).</p></section><section id="S8"><h2 class="pmc_sec_title">Determination of Genetic Fidelity</h2>
<p>Variations between regenerated and donor plants can be exhibited at phenotypic, cytological, biochemical, and genetic/epigenetic levels (<a href="#B77" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="B77">Hillig, 2005</a>; <a href="#B118" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="B118">Miguel and Marum, 2011</a>; <a href="#B169" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="B169">Smulders and de Klerk, 2011</a>; <a href="#B1" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="B1">Abreu et al., 2014</a>). These variations can be determined through different approaches, such as morphological, cytological, biochemical, and molecular analyses (<a href="#F7" class="usa-link">Figure 7</a>). For morphological traits, changes are not always observed at early developmental stages or may not entirely display the variations. By contrast, the use of cytological and molecular detection approaches determines differences at chromosomal and DNA levels, respectively, regardless of the developmental stages in various plant species (<a href="#B31" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="B31">Clarindo et al., 2012</a>; <a href="#B134" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="B134">Pathak and Dhawan, 2012</a>; <a href="#B37" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="B37">Currais et al., 2013</a>; <a href="#B1" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="B1">Abreu et al., 2014</a>; <a href="#B13" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="B13">Bello-Bello et al., 2014</a>). To date, several studies have been reported on the use of different molecular markers in <em>Cannabis</em> spp. genetic diversity, fingerprinting, etc. These markers include random amplified polymorphic DNA (RAPD), restriction fragment length polymorphisms (RFLP), amplified fragment length polymorphism (AFLP), microsatellites, inter simple sequence repeat (ISSR), short tandem repeat (STR) multiplex, and single nucleotide polymorphisms (SNPs) and PCR Allele Competitive Extension (PACE) assay (<a href="#B46" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="B46">Faeti et al., 1996</a>; <a href="#B93" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="B93">Kojoma et al., 2002</a>; <a href="#B5" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="B5">Alghanim and Almirall, 2003</a>; <a href="#B64" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="B64">Gilmore and Peakall, 2003</a>; <a href="#B70" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="B70">Hakki et al., 2003</a>; <a href="#B38" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="B38">Datwyler and Weiblen, 2006</a>; <a href="#B116" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="B116">Mendoza et al., 2009</a>; <a href="#B98" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="B98">Lata et al., 2010a</a>; <a href="#B61" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="B61">Gao et al., 2014</a>; <a href="#B41" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="B41">Dufresnes et al., 2017</a>; <a href="#B75" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="B75">Henry et al., 2018</a>). These molecular markers coupled with cytological and morphological analyses (<a href="#B1" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="B1">Abreu et al., 2014</a>) are valuable techniques to ensure the genetic stability of <em>in vitro</em> regenerated plants or <em>in vitro</em> conserved germplasm of <em>C. sativa</em>. To date, only ISSR markers have been used to confirm the genetic stability of <em>C. sativa</em> synthetic seeds during <em>in vitro</em> multiplication and storage for 6 months under different growth conditions, and <em>in vitro</em> propagated plants over 30 nodal subcultures in culture and hardening in soil for 8 months, compared to the corresponding donor plants (<a href="#B98" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="B98">Lata et al., 2010a</a>, <a href="#B101" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="B101">2011</a>).</p>
<figure class="fig xbox font-sm" id="F7"><h3 class="obj_head">FIGURE 7.</h3>
<p class="img-box line-height-none margin-x-neg-2 tablet:margin-x-0 text-center"><a class="tileshop" target="_blank" href="https://www.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/core/lw/2.0/html/tileshop_pmc/tileshop_pmc_inline.html?title=Click%20on%20image%20to%20zoom&amp;p=PMC3&amp;id=7968383_fpls-12-627240-g007.jpg"><img class="graphic zoom-in" src="https://cdn.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/pmc/blobs/fffd/7968383/21e238aa3acf/fpls-12-627240-g007.jpg" loading="lazy" height="476" width="787" alt="FIGURE 7"></a></p>
<div class="p text-right font-secondary"><a href="figure/F7/" class="usa-link" target="_blank" rel="noopener noreferrer">Open in a new tab</a></div>
<figcaption><p>A flow chart depicting different approaches that can be used to determine the genetic stability of <em>in vitro</em> regenerated or conserved cannabis plants, compared to its donor counterparts.</p></figcaption></figure></section><section id="S9"><h2 class="pmc_sec_title">Projected Contribution of Tissue Culture in the Global Cannabis Industry</h2>
<p>The present global cannabis market is worth $340 B<sup><a href="#footnote2" class="usa-link">2</a></sup>. To supply cannabis (medical and recreational) to global consumers, a stable supply chain of quality production and value-added product development still needs to be established. Considering the average annual weighted usage base of 110 g per customer (Canaccord Genuity), the global cannabis demand currently could be around 19-20 M kg per year. Major cannabis consumers are in Europe, North America, South America, Asia, and Oceanic parts of the world, with an estimate of 263 million people using the drug in the previous year (European Consumer Stables Report, 2018; World Drug Report, 2019). To produce 20 M kg of cannabis every year, considering a 40-gm yield per plant, would require 500 M clones/seeds a year. An average price of $10, as, then, the overall present global expected market size for tissue culture clones/manual clones could be predicted around $5B. With intensive indoor cultivation, tissue culture clonal planting material can also reduce the risk of fungal and viral diseases, substantially reducing production cost to under $0.5 per gram to maintain a profitable cannabis production (<a href="#T4" class="usa-link">Table 4</a>). Considering these global demand scenarios, the supply of clean cannabis clones (pest free, and true to type tested) is an important supply chain component essential for the success and future growth of cannabis industry. To sustain and support the industry growth and make the production cost-effective, optimization in the cannabis tissue culture technology is vital.</p>

<div class="p text-right font-secondary"><a href="table/T4/" class="usa-link" target="_blank" rel="noopener noreferrer">Open in a new tab</a></div></section><p>The <em>in vitro</em> propagation of cannabis is superior to conventional methods because of disease-free elite plants’ production and a high multiplication rate. The cannabis industry is keen to invest in <em>in vitro</em> propagation due to (i) saving footprint/production area by shifting a mother room to a tissue culture lab that will be almost 10% the size of the space needed same number of clones.</p>
<p>The main hurdle of <em>in vitro</em> propagation is the capital cost for the tissue culture lab setup. Setting up a massive large-scale production facility can involve a multimillion-dollar investment. Industry and technology will need to continue to improve and reduce costs so that <em>in vitro</em> propagation can be affordable for all growers.</p>
<p>In other plants, under a laminar flow hood setting, on an average of 100 plants per hour with 2000 working hours, 200,000 plants can be produced in a year. With an hourly labor cost of $35 per hour will cost around $0.35 per tissue culture plant (<a href="#B162" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="B162">Sluis, 2005</a>). This is around 60% of the production cost, adding another $0.15 for other costs (including electricity, resources, and marketing) makes it a baseline cost of $0.50 per plant. Scale also makes some impact on the cost of production being larger facilities can reduce the cost per plant significantly. These production costs can be as low as $0.15 per plant if the plants are produced in India, Singapore, China, or Africa where labor costs are comparatively low.</p>
<p>A few biotech companies recently added robotic sub-culturing technology for their cannabis plantlets and developed a fully automated micropropagation system to reduce large-scale operation costs. However, the capital investment to purchase this kind of robotic system is incredibly high at this time. Automated technologies for media preparation and dispensing, photoautotrophic bioreactor systems, robotic explant handling, and cutting, transfer laser dissected explants into fresh culture media, and automated acclimatized and hardened plant packaging in future will make cannabis tissue culture industry high throughput and extremely cost-effective for assured “<em>Just In Time</em>” supply of pest free, true-to-type cannabis clones. A conceptual model for high throughput automated cannabis <em>in vitro</em> clonal mass propagation is depicted in <a href="#F8" class="usa-link">Figure 8</a>. Robotics has the potential to bring tissue culture cost down by 25% (as low as $0.15 per plant to compete with low-cost production in some parts of the world). Tissue culture automation technology is slowly progressing, and it will not only bring high-level consistent output but also reduce the cost of production as low as 20 cents per plant.</p>
<figure class="fig xbox font-sm" id="F8"><h3 class="obj_head">FIGURE 8.</h3>
<p class="img-box line-height-none margin-x-neg-2 tablet:margin-x-0 text-center"><a class="tileshop" target="_blank" href="https://www.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/core/lw/2.0/html/tileshop_pmc/tileshop_pmc_inline.html?title=Click%20on%20image%20to%20zoom&amp;p=PMC3&amp;id=7968383_fpls-12-627240-g008.jpg"><img class="graphic zoom-in" src="https://cdn.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/pmc/blobs/fffd/7968383/38ccf18daa40/fpls-12-627240-g008.jpg" loading="lazy" height="478" width="784" alt="FIGURE 8"></a></p>
<div class="p text-right font-secondary"><a href="figure/F8/" class="usa-link" target="_blank" rel="noopener noreferrer">Open in a new tab</a></div>
<figcaption><p>Integration of automation and bioreactor technologies for mass propagation in cannabis for low cost clonal multiplication at <em>in vitro</em> level.</p></figcaption></figure></section><section id="S10"><h2 class="pmc_sec_title">Conclusion</h2>
<p>The process of developing new varieties through conventional breeding can take 7–12 years, depending on crop species. The progress of cannabis breeding programs is limited due to the difficulty in maintaining selected high yielding cross-pollinated elite genotypes under field or greenhouse conditions. Therefore, tissue culture techniques are advantageous for cannabis improvement because they can facilitate high multiplication rate and production of disease-free elite plants by overcoming the problems of heterozygosity from cross-pollination. The development of new industrial hemp and medical cannabis cultivars with improved traits could be further advanced using genome editing and other precision breeding tools, combined with <em>in vitro</em> techniques for regeneration. Unfortunately, hemp and cannabis plants’ dioecious nature complicates the efforts toward the improvement of specific traits, such as resistance to pests and diseases. Therefore, with the recent legalization, calls for serious targeted efforts are required to advance the regeneration and transformation protocols aiming to enhance the quality and safety of the plants and end products.</p></section><section id="S11"><h2 class="pmc_sec_title">Author Contributions</h2>
<p>All authors listed have made a substantial, direct and intellectual contribution to the work, and approved it for publication.</p></section><section id="conf1"><h2 class="pmc_sec_title">Conflict of Interest</h2>
